homicide
this chapter deal with the approach of the criminal law to behaviour which cause or risk causing death
murder manslaughter and several other offence are discussed and one recurrent issue here is whether english law responds proportionately to the different degree of culpability manifested in case where death is caused
death and finality
for practical purpose the culpable causing of another person death may fairly be regarded a the most serious offence in the criminal calendar
there is an argument that treason is a more serious offence since it strike at the very foundation of the state and it social organization but treason is so rare that it is surely permissible to treat homicide a the most serious form of crime
the reason for this is not difficult to find the harm caused by many other crime is remediable to a degree whereas the harm caused by homicide is absolutely irremediable
even in crime of violence which leave some permanent physical disfigurement or psychological effect the victim retains his or her life and therefore the possibility of further pleasure and achievement whereas death is final
this finality make it proper to regard death a the most serious harm that may be inflicted on another and to regard a person who chooses to inflict that harm a the most culpable of offender in the absence of some excuse or justification
although many death arise from natural cause and many others from illness and disease each year see a large number of death caused by accident and also a number caused by act or omission which amount to some form of homicide in english law
for the statistic show that there were some accidental death of which some occurred in the home at work and on the road
by comparison the number of death recorded a criminal homicide wa which includes all the murder and manslaughter
this figure is relatively small when compared with many american city but that is no cause for satisfaction
there are still awkward question to be confronted
for example are we satisfied that the death recorded a homicide are in fact more culpable than all or even most of the death recorded a accident
in other word doe english criminal law pick out the most heinous form of killing a murder and manslaughter or are the boundary frozen by tradition
another question is whether the criminal law ought not to be wider in it application to activity which carry some risk of causing death than in other sphere
thus even if it would be excessive to sweep large number of the death now recorded a accident into the law of homicide there may be sufficient justification for creating or enforcing offence designed to ensure safe condition of work safe good safe building and so on
it wa argued in chapter that the criminal law ought to spread it net wider where the potential harm is greater
it will be seen that english law doe this up to a point and in the process seems to accept argument a reason for departing from several of the principle set out in chapter
requirement of criminal homicide
english law distinguishes between the offence of murder and manslaughter a we shall see but the two crime do have certain common element
it must be proved that the defendant act or omission caused the death of the victim within a year and a day
the requirement of causation in the criminal law are discussed elsewhere
the year and a day rule is a legacy of time when medical science wa so rudimentary that if there wa a substantial lapse of time between injury and death it wa unsafe to pronounce on whether the defendant conduct or some other event caused the death
nowadays the problem is quite the reverse medical science is generally able to determine whether d conduct caused the death and innovation such a machine mean that life can be prolonged for month and year in some instance
in one recent case the victim of a stabbing died two and a half year after the incident having been kept on a machine for almost the entire time
in principle it seems unjust that a homicide conviction should not be possible in such a case if all the other element can be established and only the year and a day rule stand in the way
but the rule wa confirmed by the criminal law revision committee in on the ground that a defendant should not be left in peril of a homicide conviction indefinitely
surely prosecutorial discretion should be enough to prevent unfair conviction year after the event
the rule itself is out of accord with modern medical condition and should be abolished
defining murder the inclusionary question
a the procedural context
if causing death is to be regarded a the most serious harm that can be inflicted it would seem to follow that the most blameworthy form of homicide should result in the highest sentence imposed by the court
indeed many system of criminal law impose a mandatory sentence for murder or whatever the highest form of homicide is called in that system
in some jurisdiction this is a mandatory sentence of death
in others such a those in the united kingdom it is the mandatory sentence of life imprisonment
why should this penalty be mandatory and not at the discretion of the court a in other offence
one argument in favour of the mandatory sentence of life imprisonment is that it amount to a symbolic indication of the unique heinousness of murder
it place the offender under the state control a it were for the remainder of his or her life
this is often linked with a supposed denunciatory effect the idea that the mandatory life sentence denounces murder a emphatically a possible and with a supposed general deterrent effect in declaring that there is no mitigation of sentence available for this crime
it might also be argued that the mandatory life sentence make a substantial contribution to public safety
none of these argument is notably strong let alone conclusive
the mandatory penalty doe indeed serve to mark out murder from other crime but whether the definition of murder is sufficiently refined to capture the worst killing and only the worst killing remains to be discussed below
whether the life sentence is regarded a a sufficient denunciation in society depends on the public perception of what life imprisonment mean if it is widely believed that it result in an average of nine year imprisonment the effect will be somewhat blunted
the same applies to the general deterrent argument it effectiveness depends on whether the penalty for murder affect the calculation of potential killer at all and if it doe whether life imprisonment is seen a significantly more or le severe than the alternative of a long sentence
a for public protection this depends on executive decision with regard to release it fails to take into account whether it is necessary for public protection to keep lifer in for so long
this brings the discussion to a crucial point what doe the sentence of life imprisonment mean in practice and what would be the alternative
what it mean is that the time of release from prison is determined by the home secretary on the advice of the parole board and the lord chief justice rather than by the judge at the trial
in procedural term therefore the life sentence involves a transfer of function normally it is the judge who determines the sentence or at least it upper limit since earlier release on parole may be possible whereas a life sentence entrusts that function to the executive who must first ascertain the opinion of the lord chief justice and the trial judge
the home office is not bound by those opinion and appears to depart from them frequently
in the home secretary imposed restriction on the release of person serving life for murder of and terrorist killing murder during robbery and the sadistic or sexual murder of young child fixing a minimum of twenty year imprisonment for these offender
whatever the justification for introducing this policy it serf to underline the fact that sentencing in murder case is enacted without each case being considered individually after hearing representation from the offender counsel and without a sentence announced in court and subject to appeal
at present the executive take decision to release some murderer after only a few year and to keep others in prison for extremely long period
should those decision not be taken by the court in the same way a other sentencing decision
the answer sometimes given is that murderer should be treated differently because they are particularly dangerous anyone who chooses to kill once can choose to kill again
but this argument will seem le persuasive when we have discussed case of manslaughter by reason of diminished responsibility where a murder is reduced to manslaughter the judge ha a wide sentencing discretion and may according to the fact of the case select a determinate prison sentence a hospital order or life imprisonment
those who kill and are convicted of manslaughter by reason of diminished responsibility are no le dangerous than those convicted of murder and yet the judge ha sentencing discretion in one case and not in the other
consideration of this kind have led the house of lord select committee on murder and life imprisonment to recommend the abolition of the mandatory sentence for murder
in it place would be a sentencing discretion which judge would use in many case to mark the relative heinousness of the murder by a determinate prison sentence
in case where life imprisonment wa thought to be the appropriate sentence the trial judge would still specify in open court the number of year to be spent in prison taking the gravity of the offence into account and release would be determined by a judicial tribunal
such a reform would bring improvement in natural justice without loss of public protection
b degree of homicide
english criminal law ha two degree of homicide murder and manslaughter
many american jurisdiction have three degree of murder murder requiring premeditation and and murder covering lesser shade of culpability
in england there ha been a proposal that we should do away with all these distinction leaving a single offence of criminal homicide
that proposal if implemented would shift almost all the in homicide case from the trial to the sentencing stage the only issue of any consequence in most case would be the sentence to be imposed and all the distinction now drawn at the stage of criminal liability would be reflected in the sentence alone
the jury would have no role in most homicide case since a plea of guilty would be the norm
contrast this with the position in jurisdiction where there are three or more degree of homicide plea of guilty to and murder would be le frequent since there would be the possibility of argument in court if no bargain wa struck beforehand between prosecution and defence and the defence might succeed in persuading the jury to reduce the degree of the killing
moreover the borderline between the various degree would raise question of law which would provide much opportunity for legal argument and for appeal
the result would be that killing were classified and labelled in a more refined way but at the cost of lengthy trial and mounting bill
that cost is not difficult to justify where capital punishment is the penalty for murder
where there is no capital punishment however the question of how many degree of homicide it is desirable to have call for careful weighing of the principle of fair labelling see chapter l against the cost and time argument mobilised by the policy of efficient administration see chapter m
the structure of the english law of homicide is rather strange
although there are two offence murder and manslaughter the latter includes two distinct variety voluntary manslaughter killing which would be murder but for the existence of defined extenuating circumstance and involuntary manslaughter killing for which there is no need to prove any awareness of the risk of death being caused but for which there is thought to be sufficient fault to justify criminal liability
the argument therefore tend to focus on three borderline question what is the minimum fault required for conviction of murder
what condition are needed to reduce murder to manslaughter
and what is the minimum fault required for a conviction of manslaughter
c requirement for murder
in english criminal law there are now two alternative fault requirement for murder an intent to kill or an intent to cause grievous bodily harm
what do these requirement mean
do they extend the definition of murder too far or are they too narrow
intent to kill may be regarded a the most obvious and indisputable form of fault element for murder but to some extent that depends on the meaning of intent
this ha been the subject of a number of house of lord decision and yet the definition is still not clear and settled
probably the most accurate statement would be that a person intends to kill if it is his or her purpose to kill by the act or omission charged or if he or she foresees that death is practically certain to follow from that act or omission
in this way both purpose and foresight of practical certainty are regarded a part of the definition of intent although there are other statement suggesting that foresight of practical or virtual certainty is merely evidence from which intent may be inferred
how would this test be applied
in many case the word intent is used without elaboration but there are some for which a full explanation of the meaning of intention is necessary
a fairly typical set of fact is provided by nedrick where d had a grudge against a woman and had threatened to burn her out
one night he went to her house poured paraffin through the and on to the front door and set it alight
one of the woman child died in the ensuing fire
when asked why he did it d replied just to wake her up and frighten her
a defence of this kind a claim that the purpose wa only to frighten and not to cause harm requires the full definition to be put to the jury
the question is granted that d purpose wa to frighten did he nonetheless realize that it wa practically certain that his act would cause death or grievous bodily harm to someone
the jury should answer this by drawing inference from the evidence in the case and from the surrounding circumstance
what about the alternative element in the definition an intent to cause grievous bodily harm
this ha considerable practical importance since this is all that the prosecution ha to prove in order to obtain a verdict of guilty of murder
it must be shown that the defendant intended which again includes both purpose and awareness of practical certainty to cause really serious injury to someone
the house of lord confirmed this rule in cunningham d struck his victim on the head a number of time with a chair causing injury from which the victim died a week later
d maintained throughout that he had not intended to kill but there wa evidence from which the jury could infer and did infer that he intended to cause grievous bodily harm
the house of lord upheld d conviction for murder an intent to cause really serious injury is sufficient for murder without any proof that the defendant intended or even contemplated the possibility that death would result
doe the grievous bodily harm rule extend the definition of murder too far
if the point of distinguishing murder from manslaughter is to mark out the most heinous group of killing for the extra stigma of a murder conviction it can be argued that the grievous bodily harm rule draw the line too low
in term of principle the rule requires justification because it departs from the principle of correspondence see chapter a namely that the fault element in a crime should relate to the consequence prohibited by that crime
by allowing an intent to cause grievous bodily harm to suffice for a murder conviction the law is violating a general principle turning the most serious of it offence into a constructive crime
why should it be necessary to construct a murder conviction out of this lesser intent
there are argument in favour of this death is final murder is the gravest crime and there is no significant moral difference between someone who chooses to cause really serious injury and someone who set out to kill
no one can predict whether a serious injury will result in death that may depend on the victim physique on the speed of an ambulance on the distance from the hospital and on a range of other medical and individual matter
if one person chooses to cause serious injury to another it should be presumed that he or she realizes that there is always a risk of death and such case show a sufficiently wanton disregard for life a to warrant the label murder if death result
the which would uphold the principle of correspondence are that breach of that principle is unnecessary when the amplitude of the crime of manslaughter lie beneath murder and also that the definition of grievous bodily harm includes a number of injury which are most unlikely to put the victim life at risk
in the leading case of cunningham lord dissenting gave the example of breaking someone arm that is a really serious injury but one which is unlikely to endanger the victim life
so in practice the grievous bodily harm rule go further than the argument of it protagonist would support
would it be right then to confine the fault element in murder to an intent to kill
that would have the merit of simplicity but would it strike the right note socially
there are powerful argument in favour of saying that there are other killing where an intent to kill can not be established and yet where the moral or social culpability is equal to that in most intentional killing
of course one can not be adamant about whether they merit the label murder because that is a question of drawing the line between murder and manslaughter which is not susceptible of any precise resolution
it is hard to argue conclusively that the category of murder should be smaller or larger unless the death penalty or a mandatory life sentence follows it is a question of social judgment
let u briefly consider some of the possibility
the fault element for many serious offence is intent or recklessness why should this not suffice for murder
the question is whether all killing in which the defendant is aware of the risk of death are sufficiently serious to warrant the term murder
one answer sometimes given is that they are not because a driver who overtakes on a bend knowingly taking the risk that there is a car travelling in the opposite direction should not be labelled a murderer if a collision and death happen to ensue
this example assumes that a sympathy for motorist with overwhelm any tendency to logical analysis
one might ask whether motorist are ever justified in knowingly taking risk with other people life
yet if the example is modified a little so that the overtaking is on a country road at night and the risk is known to be slight it becomes questionable whether the causing of death in these circumstance should be labelled in the same way a intentional killing
this is not to suggest that motorist should be treated differently
the point is rather that even though knowingly taking risk with other people life is usually unjustifiable taking a slight risk is le serious than intentionally causing death
in discussing the boundary of murder we are concerned with classification not exculpation
to classify all reckless killing a murder might be too broad but the point remains that some reckless killing may be thought no le heinous than intentional killing
can a satisfactory line be drawn here
one approach would be to draw the line by reference to the degree of probability
murder is committed in those situation where d caused death by an act or omission which he knew had death a the probable or highly probable result
a version of this test of foresight of high probability is used in several other european country it wa introduced into english law by the decision in hyam v
dpp but abandoned in moloney on ground of uncertainty
a second approach is to frame the law in such a way a to make it clear that the court should make a moral judgment on the gravity of the defendant conduct
section of the model penal code includes within murder those reckless killing which manifest extreme indifference to the value of human life
scot law treat a murder killing with wicked recklessness a phrase which directs the court attention to the circumstance of the killing
both the model penal code test and the scot test may be reduced to circularity however for when one asks how extreme or how wicked the recklessness should be the only possible answer is wicked or extreme enough to justify the stigma of a murder conviction
admittedly the model penal code doe contain a list of circumstance which may amount to extreme indifference which assist the court and increase the predictability of verdict in a way that scot law doe not but the essence of both approach is that there is no precise way of describing those killing which are a heinous a intentional killing
their protagonist argue that the law of murder is so important socially that derogation from the principle of maximum certainty should be allowed in favour of more accurate labelling by the court opponent argue that the principle of maximum certainty is needed here specifically to reduce the risk of verdict based on discriminatory or irrelevant factor such a distaste for the defendant background allegiance or other activity
a third more precise formulation derives from the recommendation of the criminal law revision committee in namely that a killing should be classified a murder in those situation where there is an intention to cause serious injury coupled with awareness of the risk of death
neither an intention to cause serious injury nor recklessness a to death should be sufficient on it own but together they restrict one another producing a test which both satisfies the criterion of certainty and mark out some heinous but killing
a fourth approach adopted by english law until and still in force in many american jurisdiction is some form of rule anyone who kill during the course of a felony or more restrictively a serious crime of violence or whilst resisting arrest should be convicted of murder
thus stated there is no reference to the defendant intention or awareness of the risk the fact that d ha chosen to commit rape robbery or another serious offence and ha caused death thereby is held to constitute sufficient moral ground for placing the killing in the highest category
plainly this is a form of constructive criminal liability the murder conviction is constructed out of the ingredient of a lesser offence
presumably the justification is that d ha already crossed a high threshold in choosing to commit such a serious offence and should therefore be held liable for whatever consequence ensue however unforeseeable they may be
the objection would be reduced if awareness of the risk of death wa also required in other word if the test were the commission of a serious offence of violence plus recklessness a to death
the effect of that test would be to pick out those reckless killing which occurred when d had already manifested substantial moral and legal culpability and to classify them a murder
four alternative approach have been described and many others could be listed
the point is that the traditional concept of intention and recklessness do not of themselves appear to be sufficiently well focused to mark out those killing which are the most heinous
the law must resort to some kind of moral and social evaluation of conduct if it is to identify and separate out the most heinous killing
many people might think that a person who cause death whilst using an unlawfully held firearm or explosive ought to be convicted of murder because there is generally speaking no excuse for using such dangerous equipment
some of the people thus covered would be armed robber others would be terrorist
the armed robber might say that he had no intention of using the firearm that he carried it with him simply to frighten the victim and that it went off accidentally if the jury belief that should he be convicted of murder
property do let yourself in for trouble choosing the right agent is essential if you want to rent your property
francesca simon describes some of the pitfall and how to avoid them
by francesca simon
debbie adam had to let her east end terrace house in london in a hurry when her husband wa to ireland with two month notice
she went to a local letting agency landlord of roman road bethnal green london who found a tenant that day
she also employed landlord to manage the let
a is common practice landlord kept the tenant deposit of and told debbie they would take their entire fee of up front so she expected no rent for the first month
but july also passed without debbie receiving any money
i phoned and said where the money and wa told there were problem with the who would be coming in to sort it out say debbie

in case of a dispute you can complain to the professional body and member are bonded which mean that deposit and rent are covered in case of fraud or dishonesty
if you are thinking of letting your property the following point will help to safeguard your interest

if in doubt ask to see proof of their membership

expect to pay a management fee of up to per cent
some agency deduct it every month others want it all up front so shop around
if the owner is abroad the agent will also collect tax at the standard rate of per cent which will be deducted from your monthly rent cheque

section of the estate agent act dealing with bonding licensing and standard of competency ha not been brought in

until then owner and tenant beware
travel copse and robber christopher somerville see how woodland ha usurped the ancient cattle thief of the cheviot hill
by christopher somerville
when a charlton chief sat down to dinner in time gone by and found a spur on the table where his meal should have been he knew the cupboard wa bare once more
his wife wa telling him to saddle up and steal a fresh batch of cattle from his neighbour herd
the family of north tynedale in westernmost northumberland charltons and robsons milburns and dodds were never slow to lay hand on each other property in the lawless old day
here where the scottish border ran across the cheviot hill far from civilisation reiving or wa a way of life
remoteness is still the keynote of this region but a charlton reiving party would be hard put to it these day to work up a good gallop along the upper reach of the north tyne river
since the western fringe of the cheviot have been planted with square mile of conifer while in their depth sprawl the seven mile of the kielder water reservoir europe largest lake
i found it hard to picture what kind of landscape those statistic might have shaped until i opened the ordnance survey map to find half of it coloured green with a great blue stain in the centre
kielder water is beautiful and the kielder tree formidably impressive in their domination of the hill
but by the time i had turned off the road from bellingham at kielder village and driven up the bumpy forest drive to east kielder farm i wa longing for the sight of something other than water and tree
relief wa at hand
the farm stand on a spur of moorland on the northern edge of the forest and within minute i had turned my back on the corduroy battalion of tree and wa striding under a still cloudy sky over tussock of rush and coarse grass with my face to the long bare shoulder of open hillside that flank the winding shallow of the east kielder burn
the footpath that run with the burn is not one of those carefully waymarked and leafleted by the forestry commission
there are few attraction for the kielder tourist here
the burn thread a wild and inhospitable crevice of the hill where the wind blow cold and the sense of isolation grows with each lonely mile
walking here you leave the century behind on the outskirt of the forest and enter the reconstructed emptiness in which the reivers and raider of the past could operate unhindered
i squelched across tiny burn running in black channel of peat and stood looking down from the hillside on to the grey roof of scaup farm
the hollow barking of a dog came from one of the stone shed but there wa no sign of life around the building
scaup must be just about the loneliest farm in england tucked down in the shelter of the hill with kielder forest dark mass blocking it southward view and the narrowing valley of the burn filling half the sky to the north
the east kielder burn divide here into scaup burn and white kielder burn
i dropped down the hill to ford white kielder upstream of the ruined farmstead of kielder head where a line of stunted alder shaggy with lichen leaned over their leafless reflection in the water
spring come late to this windbitten and valley
but there were lamb in the sheep pen of scaup and a cheery hello now from the farmer and his wife tending them
those were the last human voice i heard all day
life up here in the cleft of the white kielder burn
the rough land is altogether too harsh and unyielding giving sustenance only to sheep and to the conifer high on the flank of east kielder moor and grey mare knowe immense sweep of hillside to right and left of the valley
the footpath on the left bank of the burn wa little better than a sheep track but there were clue to it former importance a a droving road over the hill to byrness in redesdale
large boulder stood out beside the path a grey blob in the featureless sheet of pale grass and flat stone had been placed a bridge across the burn their back hollowed by the tread of foot through the century
following these now disregarded sign of past activity along the old path i traversed the hillside and came down to a crumbling stone sheepfold with the roofless remains of a shepherd hut built into one wall
the tiny single room wa jammed with the mossy timber of disused sheep pen but the little iron fire grate that once warmed the hardy men who worked here wa still set in the foot of the hut tottering gable end and the massive stone lintel had not yet fallen from it place above the doorway
a dipper flew up from the burn a i emerged from the hut his white breast flashing a he darted downstream
somewhere ahead in the crag of kielderhead moor a hawk wa whistling a piercing sound in the lonely valley overlain for a few moment by the snarl of a jet
that jarring manifestation of furious technology seemed a remote and ridiculous a a dream a silence settled down on kielderhead and the hawk whistle came down from the moor
the valley of the white kielder burn steepened again a i trudged north it curve tightening
now the path ran through heather high above the burn past circular sheepfold long disused and over the stony bed of side stream where the grass hung smooth and inviting concealing drop
a mile above the shepherd hut the hillside ceased their upward rolling and curved together to form a valley of slope five mile from the nearest tarmac road hidden deep in the fold of the moor
the old drover road turned aside here and climbed under the rock face of white crag to continue it lonely course over girdle fell and down into redesdale
i could have gone with it giving myself four blissful mile of upland walking followed by a many miserable hour of fruitless searching for return transport to east kielder farm
instead i lay back in the heather and savoured the trickle of the burn and the crisp smell of ground
not a sign of spring had yet penetrated this secret valley but another month would put that right
there were five mile of return tramping to face but they too could wait awhile

by greg neale environment correspondent
hundred of thousand of dolphin could be saved from death thanks to pioneering work by british scientist
they believe they have found a cheap practical mean of preventing dolphin entangling themselves in monofilament drift fishing net the wall of death believed to be responsible for killing large number of dolphin off cornwall this year

using equipment donated by racal the scientist have analysed recording of the clicking dolphin make while hunting

they emit an intense click or pulse of sound

mr goodson who became interested in the sonar capacity of dolphin after hearing one while swimming off the welsh coast ha experimented with various shape of sound reflector
the most promising is eliptical about long
the theory is that it internal air pocket will bounce back the dolphin sonar signal regardless of the direction of approach
last autumn dr klinowska mr goodson and their team put the theory into practice

the movement of dolphin around the barrier wa observed using electronic equipment to monitor sonar activity
the result exceeded our expectation said mr goodson

dr klinowska said that although it wa necessary to be cautious about the finding a combination of reflector device and some modification of fishing gear might reduce death

a paradigm of probation practice some sobering thought singer
lawrence singer is an assistant chief probation officer with merseyside probation service
beforehand he worked a a probation officer for berkshire probation service and later a a research officer for somerset probation service where he undertook the research reported here
the paradigm which is described originates from doctoral research into generic probation practice and ha been presented in a book adult probation and juvenile supervision beyond the dilemma gower
summary
the government white paper crime justice and protecting the public proposes a radical change for probation practice
henceforth probation work will no longer be an alternative to punishment but an alternative form of punishment
this article challenge the conceptual and practical appropriateness of applying the notion of punishment to the probation context
probation officer it is argued are concerned with establishing rule for probationer and not inflicting pain on them
an alternative paradigm of probation practice is described
the paradigm is based on the joint supervisory aim of support and surveillance which are implemented through the method of counselling assisting directing and monitoring
to illustrate the paradigm reference is made to an alcohol education course developed for offender in trouble through drink
the efficacy of the alcohol project and the paradigm is demonstrated by the result reported from a three year evaluation study covering offender
the article concludes with an affirmation of the disciplined but character of current probation work and argues that to successfully protect the public and reintegrate offender into the community the probation officer employment of established social work knowledge skill and value is a strength not a weakness
the aim of this article is to challenge the assertion contained in the white paper crime justice and protecting the public home office that probation practice can be conceived of a a form of punishment in the community
issue will be taken with the appropriateness of applying the concept of punishment to the probation context through reference to a demanding and successful probation project concerned with offender in trouble through drink
in describing the project a model for understanding probation practice will be developed
this paradigm will provide a framework for contrasting the efficacy of current practice with future plan inappropriately to transform probation work into a new approach to punishment in the community para
the punitive approach anticipated for the probation service in the white paper is underpinned by a definition of punishment that resides in the restriction on liberty and in the enforcement of the order
the white paper go on to emphasize it is the loss of liberty involved in carrying out the term of the order rather than the activity carried out during the order which is the punishment para
notwithstanding the popular appeal and political capital to be derived from such a statement this definition is nevertheless at variance with the traditional understanding of the meaning of punishment
discourse on punishment and dictionary definition of the term clearly specify the central feature of punishment a being the infliction of pain rather than the loss of liberty per se hudson
the conceptual and practical implication of this discrepancy will be examined in the third and final section of this article
beforehand however consideration will be given first to the probation project and second to it evaluation
in these section punishment will be understood to mean the infliction of pain and the review of the project will seek to demonstrate that the probation practice described is not only more demanding but also more effective than this traditional understanding of punishment
at a time when the home secretary is inviting the probation service to move centre stage in the criminal justice system nacro it may seem misguided and unnecessarily pedantic to take issue with the white paper notion of punishment
punishment is just a word after all nothing more than convenient political rhetoric meriting only from probation manager and practitioner poised at the window of opportunity
however this fails to acknowledge a fundamental axiom of social work practice namely that language not only reflects but also shape social reality
a davy ha pointed out speech is the basic tool of the social worker
what we say influence what we think and in turn what we do
if punishment is assimilated into the probation glossary it will inevitably influence the sort of practice the service undertakes
in the section to follow the stark question is therefore put is the probation service in the business of inflicting pain and the answer which unfolds may be summarized no because it neither aspires nor wish to
the project more demanding than punishment
government concern about the problem of offending is a old a the probation service
indeed a bochel ha shown the establishment of a probation service in england and wale wa inextricably linked with work undertaken with offender in trouble through drink
almost a century later the new approach to punishment in the community outlined by the white paper once again present alcohol misuse a a key problem requiring intervention
alcohol the white paper state is implicated in a wide variety of crime drunken driving offence of public disorder and domestic violence para
in describing it vision of effective supervision the white paper go on to note
offender take part in alcohol education programme so that they can learn to drink sensibly
they learn about the relative strength of different drink the amount which is safe to consume and the pitfall to avoid competitive drinking and buying round para
a a summary of alcohol education programme this is something of a simplification
however it doe provide a useful introduction to the origin of the alcohol education course reported here
furthermore it highlight the suitability of referring to this type of project for testing out the appropriateness of applying the notion of punishment to probation practice
in the management of somerset probation service wa faced with the fact that nearly one in three of the offender supervised in their area were either known to be or suspected of misusing alcohol singer
aware of practitioner frustration in not being able to work effectively with these problematic client probation management in partnership with the voluntary sector determined to develop an alcohol education course aec
by when social inquiry report monitoring information covering offender indicated that the problem of offending wa virtually a great for a for client that is alcohol wa cited a a problem in per cent of case a specialist project worker had been appointed
towards the end of an aec wa launched and ha been running ever since
the aim of the course is to educate offender about alcohol to facilitate a lasting change in their subsequent use of this drug
this aim is underpinned by three interrelated objective and it is not without significance how they complement and serve to implement the white paper own trinity of aim of supervision that is protection of the public prevention of reoffending and successful reintegration of the offender in the community para
these were

to increase knowledge about alcohol so that future alcohol use can be more informed and therefore more prudent

to instil or reinforce responsible drinking attitude and behaviour

to encourage the maintenance of or where appropriate a reduction to a personally and socially safe level of alcohol unit intake
to meet these objective the aec ha a structured programme involving group and individual contact that is six group session sandwiched between interview held immediately before and after the group
the initial meeting held with one of the two group leader serf to clarify what participant can expect from the course and what is expected of them
a standard contract which cover condition of attendance and an agreement to complete various assignment between group session is signed by each participant
the final interview entail a personalized review of the course a well a the formulation of a plan to be undertaken with the offender supervising officer
the course employ a variety of material and technique including the use of checklist magazine and newspaper clipping quiz video brainstorm exercise discussion drink diary and role play menary
the aec is primarily intended for young adult subject to a probation order with a special condition of attendance schedule ii criminal justice act and reflects the fact that most somerset offender in trouble through drink commit serious offence posse a substantial number of previous conviction
in short they are seriously at risk of committing further offence and becoming trapped in the revolving door of custody
the development of the somerset aec both accord with and constitutes a practical example of the home office recent guidance to sentencers
in recent year the probation service ha devoted increasing resource to supervising the more serious and persistent offender
probation order which are combined with constructive disciplined and effective programme of supervision are increasingly being used for offender who stand to receive custodial sentence
from the description of the aec already given the demanding and disciplined nature of the course can be appreciated
however the course is not punitive it is not concerned with the infliction of pain
instead it is concerned with the establishment of rule
this is plainly evident from the section of the contract signed by each participant where what is expected of them is spelt out
you will be expected to keep a drink diary throughout the course and make attempt at reducing the amount you drink if it is greater than accepted healthy drinking level
you must be willing to be helped and help others your experience will be useful to other group member
you must attend session these will consist of an introductory session six group session and a final assessment interview
you must not drink before the session
you must be on time lateness will count a an absence
unlike the prison inmate aec participant can not sit back and passively do their time
probation practice is not however solely concerned with the establishment of rule
a ha been demonstrated elsewhere with regard to the generic probation setting singer the supervision of offender involves two overarching aim namely support and surveillance
support refers to anything which is said or done to help the offender solve or mitigate their personal or social problem surveillance to anything which is said or done to induce the offender to conform to socially acceptable standard of behaviour
these aim are implemented through the practice of four distinguishable but related method
in term of support there is counselling and assisting in term of surveillance there is directing and monitoring
each of these four method are employed during the eight session
although summarized a an alcohol education course the session are not just concerned with giving information but also with enabling participant to make practical use of it
in the first group session the counselling method is registered in the course tutor note menary by the explanation this session aim to get participant to consider the subject of alcohol in way they had not thought of in the past
during this session the importance of monitoring is also emphasized
drink exercise introduce the notion of measurement concentration of alcohol content information which will be necessary to carry out the recording of alcohol consumption throughout the duration of the course
in the fifth group session entitled focus on the individual the counselling and the monitoring combine with the directing and assisting method
at the start of the session the tutor note make clear that directing extends beyond enforcing the condition of attendance
this session allows for an in depth examination of the drinking diary over the last five week identifying the risky circumstance or situation
the aim at the end of the session is to encourage participant to make rule for their future drinking which will lead to a lessening of identified problem
similarly the assisting extends beyond simply providing handout of useful information
it is important that participant are provided with the skill to reduce their drinking level or handle more effectively pub centred problem
the use of role play incorporating previously discussed difficulty homework assignment session in real or set up situation provides an opportunity for testing these skill
probably the best illustration of the assisting method in action during the course come in the fifth session when somerset practitioner take the logic of working with the problem in the community to it ultimate conclusion
in the second half of the session course leader sponsor participant to an evening at the pub for the purpose of practising drinking skill and purchasing drink
the counselling assisting directing and monitoring on the aec give substance to the aim of support and surveillance and provide the basis for establishing a model of probation practice
punishment ha no place in this model
instead the aec can best be understood a an example of a paradigm of probation practice
twenty year ago the advisory council on the penal system observed that notion of punitiveness do not accord with the british concept of probation a an alternative to rather than a suspension of punishment quoted in bochel
twenty year later the aec demonstrates that this remains no le true now than it wa then
but doe the aec work
bringing fossil back to life
in this chapter some of the way in which palaeontologist determine the way fossil animal lived are described reanimating the dead fragment to build up a living creature
since popular idea of life in the past are often founded on vividly coloured reconstruction of the world in the jurassic period and the like it is important to remember that these imaginative scene are all inference from bone and similar fragment
in fact there is nothing fixed about such interpretation because the way the fossil are understood may change over the year but it is usually some time before new discovery percolate into the popular presentation
even the most dinosaur may have changed their habit in the last few year
many different line of evidence may be used to flesh out the bare bone of the fossil
the first of these is the evidence of the rock from which the remains were recovered
the sediment themselves reveal much about the environment of deposition a wa shown in chapter
it is important to establish whether the fossil animal actually lived in the environment which furnished it sedimentary cover or whether it remains were swept in from some other place
fortunately it is usually easy to spot such intruder
the type of sediment and the associated fossil will show whether the environment wa marine freshwater or terrestrial providing the basic information into which the ecology of the animal ha to be accommodated
the sediment themselves may have preserved some of the track left by the animal to give direct evidence of it past activity
from the track alone it is possible to be certain of the bipedal stance of certain dinosaur and to measure their stride
the character of the rock and their setting in the past geography at the time when they accumulated diagnosis the climatic setting in which the extinct fauna lived and climate imposes certain restraint on possible mode of life
savannah animal differ from those of tropical rain forest and these again from inhabitant of the tundra at high latitude
looking at the fossil animal itself the first necessity is to reconstruct it a accurately a possible from it fragmentary remains
sometimes this is a very complex business particularly for vertebrate with number of small bone
to proceed from the reconstruction to an assessment of probable life habit two different but complementary approach are used
one method attempt to compare the structure of teeth or limb or some other feature of the extinct animal with living analogue
these do not have to be biologically related organism
the basic argument is that structure that are closely similar were probably adapted to a similar function
sometimes these similarity are quite obvious the ferocious teeth of a predatory dinosaur are a sure indication of hunting habit with hardly a glance at the fang of living mammalian carnivore
but the structure and functioning of grinding or chewing teeth in other mammal in which opposing teeth in action and which can be matched in extinct unrelated mammal is a much more subtle matter involving detailed study on the operation of living dental system to help elucidate the functioning of fossil one
the technique of hunt the analogue is a favourite one practised by palaeontologist but it is certainly not a foolproof one because there are many fossil animal that defy comparison with living organism and some analogue do not stand up to detailed scrutiny
the second method try to analyse the structure of the fossil almost a if it were a piece of engineering
if the fossil is constructed in a certain way then there are only a limited number of job that the structure could perform
the idea here is to decide which of the possibility is the most likely one
this assumes that nature only manufacture efficient design
for the most part this is a reasonable assumption to make
most animal today do seem to have body that accord well with the function they have to carry out to survive flyer are aerodynamically efficient active swimmer have suitable streamlining herbivorous mammal have teeth appropriate for grinding plant food and so on
ideally one could construct a model of the fossil to test out these various function in experiment but the number of example where the analysis ha been pursued this far are limited
of course it can never be known whether the right answer ha been reached unless somebody dredge up a living fossil from the depth of the ocean there are only varying degree of probability
but the best answer are probably obtained where the functional design of the fossil point to the same life habit a a living analogue and where both are consistent with the geological circumstance in which the fossil remains occur
swimming trilobite
there are thousand of different kind of trilobite
all of them were marine and all of them are extinct
since many kind of trilobite at any one time they were probably occupying different ecological niche behaving in different way corresponding with the wide variation in shape that they show
we can use the method described above to elucidate some of these occupation and get a glimpse albeit an imperfect one of the trilobite a it lived
i will take an example where all the method can be used and where all point in the same direction so that the answer is probably correct
most trilobite were broadly being rather longer than wide not greatly convex and with eye that occupy perhaps a quarter of the length of the head
there are a few trilobite specie however with enormous globular eye see below
by examining the way these animal were put together it is possible to suggest a likely mode of life
ordovician trilobite seen from the top and side
twice it natural size
enlargement show a section through the lens to show the direction of the field of view
we can take the eye first
the trilobite eye wa of a compound type and each lens wa made up of a calcite crystal
from the optical property of this mineral we know that the lens wa able to interpret light coming from a direction more or le at to the lens surface
so we can deduce what the field of vision of our trilobite wa by looking at the direction in which all the lens face
it turn out that our animal wa able to see in almost every direction upwards downwards sideways and forward and even backwards because the eye bulged out beyond the line of the rest of the body
most other trilobite have a predominantly lateral field of view
if the animal lived on the sea bottom it seems unlikely that it would have eye lens specialized for looking downwards and we begin to suspect that the animal habitually dwelt above the sea floor
other feature of it shape are consistent with this
at edge of the eye are a pair of long spine and these point downwards at a steep angle at very awkward attitude for resting comfortably on the sea floor
most normal trilobite have a more or le horizontal rim around the forward margin which may have rested on the sediment surface
the thorax of the trilobite is remarkably long compared with the average trilobite compare fig
the convex middle part of the thorax contained the musculature that operated the appendage which a usual are not preserved and the relatively large volume of this region show that the musculature wa powerful
the construction of this trilobite suggests that it lived an actively swimming life well above the bottom and possibly in the surface water of the sea
of course because there are no living trilobite we can not find direct confirmation of this hypothesis but we can look for other arthropod that have the same modification of the eye for example in the present day ocean
the analogy is found in such amphipod a kind of shrimp a cystosoma which also ha enormously expanded eye looking like headlamp compared with it relative see above
in fact many of the arthropod that inhabit the water column have large globular eye of this kind

living crustacean cystosoma
if it is correct that our trilobite lived above the sea bottom actively swimming in the water there are certain prediction we can make about it geological occurrence which can be tested by looking in the rock
most trilobite preferred to live at a particular water depth or on a particular type of sea bottom mud sand or lime
no such restriction should apply to our specie it should be found with all other different kind of trilobite assemblage without preference
this ha been proved in several place in some arctic locality it is found with trilobite that lived at great depth in the mud of the ordovician ocean while in canada the same specie occurs mixed with the inhabitant of the sea where limestone were accumulating
by the same token the trilobite may be expected to have a very wide geographical distribution for ocean would be no barrier to it
and again this prof to be the case our example is found in arctic canada in the high arctic island of spitsbergen thousand of mile away in what is now the desert country of nevada and utah in the united state in western ireland in russia and in northwestern australia
so in this case the different source of evidence all point in the same direction whether derived from a detailed consideration of the way the trilobite is constructed or by analogy with living animal with similar adaptation or from the evidence of the rock or the distribution of the fossil
these trilobite of the ordovician were active swimmer in the surface water a near certainly a we can ever know
there are all kind of different question we can ask about the life habit of these trilobite which are not subject to the same kind of careful scrutiny
what did they eat for example
because we do not have the leg preserved it is not possible to see how the mouth appendage functioned in feeding and neither are the stomach content preserved
so here there are only more or le likely speculation
active swimmer in the surface water of the present ocean are likely to feed directly on the plankton and the trilobite may have had a method of harvesting large quantity of minute food
or they may have been hunter after larger prey in which case when appendage are eventually discovered they may prove to have adaptation for grasping and manipulating larger food
puzzle remain even though we can be certain of the rudiment of the story
controversy among the giant dinosaur
the giant sauropod dinosaur like brachiosaurus were the largest land animal the world ha seen they weighed more than tonne and present quite different problem in the interpretation of their life habit from the diminutive trilobite
such spectacular animal have obviously attracted much attention and one might suppose that the problem of how they lived would have been satisfactorily solved long ago
most of the popular book showing the mesozoic giant in their natural setting portray them wallowing about in swamp flanked by deep vegetation their body largely under water
surely an animal of this bulk it wa argued must be partly supported by water and their relatively inconsequential teeth must have been adapted for chewing on the kind of soft luxuriant vegetation that flourish in and around swamp
because their nasal opening were on the top of their head they could even continue to breathe if it became necessary see to totally submerge
recently the life habit of the giant have been looked at in a way that disproves most of these traditional notion
consider the structure of their leg
the sauropod have relatively long leg resembling those of the elephant the largest living land animal and may have been well adapted for supporting the huge bulk of the animal
the foot of the sauropod are small relatively speaking with short stubby toe yet animal that walk on soft mud tend to have spreading foot to distribute their weight more evenly
it is difficult to see how the compact foot of the sauropod could avoid becoming stuck fast in the soft muddy bottom of a lake
if the dinosaur did after all live on dry land then the long neck could have usefully functioned to allow the animal to browse the high foliage of tree see
it ha been suggested that an animal of the size of brachiosaurus could not have breathed under water because of the pressure on the lung
their fossil remains seem to occur with other animal and plant which are generally accepted a terrestrial
there is even some evidence from the track they have left behind that diplodocus and it ally moved about in herd
they may have been the gigantic reptilian analogue of the elephant and it may be no coincidence that the elephant also ha it nasal opening on top of the skull with the nostril in this case sited at the end of the trunk it ha been suggested that some sauropod may have had a proboscis of some sort
the balance of evidence seems to be swinging away from the original idea of giant dinosaur to fully terrestrial habit
but other aspect of the dinosaur living habit are still more strongly debated
in the last few year a powerful controversy ha arisen over whether the dinosaur a a whole were like all living reptile and there is no doubt that dinosaur were reptile or resembling mammal in this respect
animal have to warm up before they can be fully active that is why lizard and snake bask in the sun in temperate climate
for this reason they can not cope with climate having greatly extended winter
animal have the same body temperature at all time and can be more continuously active but they use far more energy and hence need more food than animal of the same size
the posture of many dinosaur and particularly the carnivorous theropod dinosaur wa fully erect with the leg beneath the body and unlike the sprawling leg of living reptile see
the long back leg of such hunter look highly suitable for running and a they did so the long tail may have been held erect a a kind of see
for any kind of prolonged activity would have been a distinct advantage
under the microscope even the bone structure of these dinosaur look more like that of living mammal than reptile
on the other hand it can be argued that the sauropod like brachiosaurus were so large and with a relatively small surface area through which to cool compared with their enormous volume that their cooling rate could have been slow enough to allow them to sustain more continuous activity than smaller living reptile
it ha also been argued that the small mouth of the giant simply could not have downed enough food to support a metabolism particularly plant food that need a lot of processing before it becomes available a energy
the armoured or plated dinosaur include some specie that seem too heavily burdened with protective armour to have been very active but this doe not necessarily mean that they had to be
protagonist of the theory will show some of the horned dinosaur charging over the cretaceous plain like furious reptilian rhinoceros
one idea about the life habit of the dinosaur giant published in joc and colette at the natural history museum in
the supporter tend to ally themselves with the supporter of a theory about the relationship of the dinosaur
this is that the dinosaur or a close dinosaur relative included the ancestor of the living bird
some of the smaller and most certainly highly active dinosaur were about the size of a chicken and there is more than a passing similarity between a running ostrich and the kind of reconstruction that show running dinosaur
the point is that bird themselves are like mammal and if bird and dinosaur are a closely related a now seems likely then it obviously increase the likelihood that the dinosaur themselves may have been
the pro and con of this theory are the subject of much contentious debate by expert but it would probably be correct to say that the greater number of specialist believe that at least the more bipedal of the dinosaur including small and large carnivore were active animal
a lot of the argument among the authority on these animal is about the equivalence or otherwise of certain bone in dinosaur and bird skeleton and particularly the jurassic bird archaeopteryx this make for rather dry reading for the layman
but the is the accurate reconstruction of the jurassic and cretaceous terrestrial world over which the dinosaur were indisputably dominant
action and inaction in the city
susanne macgregor and ben pimlott
the essay in this book do not amount to a programme but they are intended to provide a springboard for one
the aim is to analyse a problem which economic growth alone ha failed to cure and to consider possible new form of public action
the biggest single difficulty in drawing attention to urban poverty is that it is not new but simply in some of it most worrying manifestation getting worse
like the poor themselves the inner city ha long been with u
ever since the industrial revolution created a mass urban society the condition of the poorest city dweller have given rise to anxiety among the better off
the inner city ha been variously regarded a a cause for moral outrage a threat to public order or a a stain on the nation conscience
meanwhile bad housing bad sanitation lack of education and other perennial ill associated with overcrowding and poverty have been routinely denounced by political leader and social reformer
a central objective of the welfare state wa indeed to alleviate the problem of the urban poor over whom beveridge five giant want disease ignorance squalor and idleness had for so long held sway
in the early and the belief wa widely held that a combination of government intervention in the form of town planning housing and health programme and the provision of social security and permanent male full employment together with an increase in real wage would reduce suffering due to poverty to manageable proportion
the labour government wa elected on a tide of impatience at the slackening pace of social reform but also of optimism that little more than economic growth fuelled by technological change wa needed to remove the main cause of urban deprivation macgregor
disillusion swiftly followed
the work of peter townsend and others showed that far from presiding over the elimination of poverty the wilson government actually failed to prevent some of it feature from worsening townsend and bosanquet
evidence began to mount that much of the social welfare paternalism of the period had created almost a many problem a it solved
neither the butler education act of nor the introduction of comprehensive school prevented a high proportion of child from
reconstruction and housing programme seemed to do nothing to prevent and seemed even to encourage a rise in crime and mental illness
meanwhile in many part of the country a problem a old a city themselves tension between cultural group between earlier immigrant and newer one between black brown and white and increasingly between large section of urban youth and the police came to be identified a the key issue
rising unemployment turned a lingering and growing malaise into a crisis
a in the united state so in britain it wa an outbreak of violence that dramatically brought the plight of the inner city to public notice
in the early at a time when manufacturing industry wa collapsing and the outlook for the unskilled looked particularly bleak frustration boiled over in a series of riot in london bristol birmingham liverpool and elsewhere
all were spontaneous outburst none contrary to some claim were politically inspired or orchestrated except in northern ireland where urban problem existed on a grander more devastating scale and where there wa a unique heritage
many had an ethnic dimension in the sense that many of the rioter were black
unlike however the notting hill riot few of those involved were immigrant the vast majority were british born
and the disturbance could not strictly be called racial the most prominent feature wa that of violent clash between young people and the police
the problem however were not confined to violent upheaval of this kind
paul harrison inside the inner city life under the cutting edge focused on hackney a london borough ranked among the worst off on any scale of urban deprivation harrison
harrison describes the way in which the inner urban lead to a vicious circle with many victim but few individual villain
the police force face the virtually impossible task of keeping the lid on the explosive mixture of ingredient that the dynamic of british society have assembled in the inner city
this mixture heated by recession and high unemployment inevitably generates a high level of crime
this necessitates in turn a far more numerous and ubiquitous police presence than in other kind of area far more frequent unpleasant contact with the public a potential suspect and far greater opportunity for police misjudgment or abuse
the inevitable sufferer of course are the poor of whom those who actually commit the offence that concern the police are a tiny minority and whom indeed it should be the purpose of the police to protect
not only are the most disadvantaged on the receiving end of most crime argues harrison they also have to put up with the heaviest police presence
they are more likely to be stopped and searched and to be the victim of mistaken arrest or conviction
at the same time the resident of the poorest area have to suffer in a way that is not true of those who live elsewhere a growing fear of attack on themselves or their property
improved police method might help writes harrison to contain the problem
but without other change the result is likely to be a society of barricaded and a steady erosion of civil liberty
and he concludes of all the warning that the inner city ha to offer for the direction british society is taking this is perhaps the most sinister

the basic social contract whereby citizen enjoy certain right including health care decent education and housing and a job in return for which they observe the rule of law wa breaking down
a a consequence an underclass wa evolving football hooligan mugger inner city rioter somewhat more frightening than their dickensian forebear because they were mobile and all too visible
recently of course there ha been an added dimension part inflated part real drug

the early witnessed a radical change in pattern of heroin taking alongside the global increase
smoking heroin chasing the dragon wa one feature of the upsurge

for the moment however the pattern of drug abuse in britain remains a varied a ever with drug such a amphetamine a serious a problem a those that hit the headline
it is a symptom of public anxiety about urban squalor and le creditably of class and racial prejudice that drug and inner city are automatically linked in the popular consciousness macgregor
nevertheless and more particularly the disruptive social consequence of addiction are especially serious in area of city poverty burr
in short if life wa always harder in the inner city than elsewhere and if condition may temporarily have been alleviated by the impact of the welfare state and rising income the last decade ha been widely represented a a period of deterioration
moreover few would dispute that this apparent decline is a reality the first word of the prime minister herself after the result of the election wa known were an acknowledgement of the challenge posed to government by the inner city
what ha been lacking however a well a the political will to do something ha been any shared agreement on the cause
can the problem of urban poverty be blamed on individual pathology
on feckless parent and grandparent
or on the social or physical environment the replacement of companionably unhygienic slum by soulless tower block with broken lift coleman et al
